<div id="review_3207371607" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3207371607" />
    <a title="DivyaJyoti Rajdev" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11651752-divyajyoti-rajdev"><img alt="DivyaJyoti Rajdev" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545022366p2/11651752.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3207371607?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DivyaJyoti Rajdev" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DivyaJyoti Rajdev" href="/user/show/11651752-divyajyoti-rajdev">DivyaJyoti Rajdev</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3207371607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10143098850321059021">Do not read this book seeking answers, for it leaves you with more questions to contemplate than answers.<br/><br/>I enjoyed Devdutt's retelling of the Mahabharata by including the folk tales and local variations of the stories. The language is simple and accessible -- unlike the original epic which as painful and dense to read as Iliad. It's easy to get lost in the twists and turns of the story, so it was particularly helpful to have the author's chapter end notes highlighting the values within the plot </span>
  <span id="freeText10143098850321059021" style="display:none">Do not read this book seeking answers, for it leaves you with more questions to contemplate than answers.<br /><br />I enjoyed Devdutt's retelling of the Mahabharata by including the folk tales and local variations of the stories. The language is simple and accessible -- unlike the original epic which as painful and dense to read as Iliad. It's easy to get lost in the twists and turns of the story, so it was particularly helpful to have the author's chapter end notes highlighting the values within the plot points.<br /><br />A few things that stood out in the book were:<br />- The commentary on the status of women. At places they're shown as lustful creatures, and virtuous &amp; pious in others. Some choose their partners, impose conditions in their domestic unions, and others are gambled / bartered away for pride / favors / political gain. <br />- The definition of Dharma. So many pivotal points in the story are driven by one having to fulfill their dharma or duty or someone failing to fulfill their dharma. Compound this with the stories that show that duty is not just good intentions, those too can have repercussions. It makes one wonder what exactly then is living by dharma. It seems to mean to act out of love, not from a place of fear or ego/self. And not be attached to the outcome. Only these acts of dharma do not accumulate karma, and allow one to remain at peace even in war.<br />- The idea of what makes someone a king or brahmin. Caste system in India is abhorrent to say the least, so I was shocked to see Yudhisthira's reply to "What makes a Brahmin" as "Understanding of the Veda" i.e. knowledge of the dharma and truth. Not the birth rank as it is commonly assumed to be. Similarly, an ideal king is a capable king. Not necessarily the one ordained by birthright.<br />- The cost of God's love. Yes Krishna protected the Pandavas and helped their spiritual growth, but he also asked them to make difficult and terrible choices that suited his own agenda. Makes you wonder if a godless existence like the Kauravas is better if they end up in heaven and the Krishna loyalists end up in hell. But then again, did the Pandavas go to hell because of what they did or because they were attached to their anger and the pride of it all?<br />- The concept of multiple Indras. When I as a Hindu think of Indra, I think of one Indra, one deity, one identity. But there seem to be many Indras who have come and gone. It is a rank, not an identity. Similarly for Brahma, there are many and each more powerful than the previous. There's a certain poetry to realizing this because not only is the world cyclical but even Godhood is, the ranks come and go. <br /><br />Having grown up watching on television the "perfect" versions of the heroes, their wives and mothers, and teachers -- it was refreshing to get an honest glimpse into their humanity. I chuckled audibly when in chapter notes of fantastical tales of virgin or occult births, Devdutt explains it away as tales spun to cover up indiscretions. God help christianity if this guy ever writes a book with chapter notes about it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10143098850321059021" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3334054171" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3334054171" />
    <a title="Sonali Dabade" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50823437-sonali-dabade"><img alt="Sonali Dabade" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590780883p2/50823437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3334054171?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sonali Dabade" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sonali Dabade" href="/user/show/50823437-sonali-dabade">Sonali Dabade</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19199728-jaya">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50823437-sonali-dabade?shelf=2020-reads">2020-reads</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3334054171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15606595117686775044">I'm so glad I decided to pick this book up! The author has explained the Mahabharata and its concepts in such a simple manner that it's a delight to read. It has made me want to delve deeper into the Indian epic and want to learn more about life and death and everything in between. The author's reasoning makes so much sense! And I know that I will reread this book because I want to make sure I understand and remember it better.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Hnfq6GbjWCc7hYWIr96/SdrqsOzynFTTF2u3ejf66dVt8IDxVEgM1foHbXGInmH2VSGRHC3KDDUJdNYLdJQjLg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Nar<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText735782282600398929\" style=\"display:none\">A reimagining of the world-famous Indian epic, the Mahabharat—told from the point of view of an amazing woman.Relevant to today’s war-torn world, The Palace of Illusions takes us back to a time that is half history, half myth, and wholly magical. Narrated by Panchaali, the wife of the legendary Pandavas brothers in the Mahabharat, the novel gives us a new interpretation of this ancient tale. The novel traces the princess Panchaali\'s life, beginning with her birth in fire and following her spirited balancing act as a woman with five husbands who have been cheated out of their father’s kingdom. Panchaali is swept into their quest to reclaim their birthright, remaining at their side through years of exile and a terrible civil war involving all the important kings of India. Meanwhile, we never lose sight of her strategic duels with her mother-in-law, her complicated friendship with the enigmatic Krishna, or her secret attraction to the mysterious man who is her husbands\' most dangerous enemy. Panchaali is a fiery female redefining for us a world of warriors, gods, and the ever-manipulating hands of fate.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"735782282600398929\" href=\"#\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10801247954235750524">Dr. Devdutt Pattanaik (born December 11, 1970) is an Indian physician turned leadership consultant, mythologist and author whose works focus largely on the areas of myth, mythology, and also management. He has written a number of books related to Hindu mythology, including Myth = Mithya: A Handbook of Hindu Mythology, a novel, The Pregnant King, and Jaya: An Illustrated Retelling of the Mahabharat</span>
  <span id="freeText10801247954235750524" style="display:none">Dr. Devdutt Pattanaik (born December 11, 1970) is an Indian physician turned leadership consultant, mythologist and author whose works focus largely on the areas of myth, mythology, and also management. He has written a number of books related to Hindu mythology, including Myth = Mithya: A Handbook of Hindu Mythology, a novel, The Pregnant King, and Jaya: An Illustrated Retelling of the Mahabharata (2010).<br />He is the Chief Belief Officer of Future Group, one of India’s largest retailers, bringing the wisdom of Indian mythology into Indian business, especifically in human resource management. He also writes a column for the newspaper MID DAY.<br />He has also written a novel based on a tale from the Mahabharata titled 'The Pregnant King' published by Penguin Books India</span>
  <a data-text-id="10801247954235750524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

This seems a deliberate souring of an uplifting narrative. Rams refusal to remarry to produce a royal heir adds to the complexity. The intention seems to be to provoke thought on notions of fidelity, property and self-image.\n\nAnd so the mythologist and illustrator Devdutt Pattanaik retells the Ramayana, drawing attention to the many oral, visual and written retellings composed in different times, in different places, by different poets, each one trying to solve the puzzle in its own unique way. \n\nThis book approaches Ram by speculating on Sita: her childhood with her father, Janaka, who hosted sages mentioned in the Upanishads; her stay in the forest with her husband, who had to be a celibate ascetic while she was in the prime of her youth; her interactions with the women of Lanka, recipes she exchanged, emotions they shared; her connection with the earth, her mother, and with the trees, her sisters; her role as the Goddess, the untamed Kali as well as the demure Gauri, in transforming the stoic prince of Ayodhya into God.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10962127631626571864\" href=\"#\"
